        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f7f678cd-27ca-451c-a08d-c29104172bdf'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d126986355" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Carbonaceous materials have been considered the most promising anode in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their low cost, good electrical conductivity, and structural stability. The main challenge of carbonaceous anodes prior to their commercialization is low initial coulomb efficiencies, derived from a lack of an efficient technique to reveal a fundamental comprehension of sodium storage mechanisms. Here, the direct observation of quasi-Na metallic clusters in carbonaceous anodes during cycling through in situ XRD is reported. By means of such a technique, a strong self-adsorption behavior forming quasi-Na metallic clusters is detected within a rationally designed highly defective ultrathin carbon nanosheets (HDCS) anode. Such a self-adsorption and crystalline system transformation mechanism in HDCS brings capacity retention about 100% after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a new principle for designing high-performance carbon anodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410673-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410673-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have significantly advanced the electric vehicle field owing to their lightweight characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0001_R_d126986011e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Nature">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the limited availability and uneven distribution of lithium resources have led to a rise in lithium costs, substantially constraining its future growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0002_R_d126986011e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Energy Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the currently available anode materials, the carbonaceous materials stand out due to their abundance from various sources, low cost-effectiveness, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0003_R_d126986011e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Albeit commercial graphite anodes have demonstrated higher capacity and long cycle life in LIBs, they fall short in SIBs due to the instability of sodium-graphite intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0004_R_d126986011e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Science Science Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, the development of a carbon material anode with high-capacity and extended lifespan holds great potential for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0005_R_d126986011e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The other two types of sodium storage carbon carbonaceous materials mainly include soft carbon and hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0006_R_d126986011e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Soft carbon anode has the advantages of high ionic conductivity, stable electrochemical properties, and minimal electrode expansion. However, the limited capacity hampers commercial viability due to low defects, a less developed pore structure, and a capacity contribution primarily originating from the slope region. Hard carbon anode is the most promising anode material due to its high capacity and cost-effectiveness in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0007_R_d126986011e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, its low initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) and rate performance have impeded its advancement for high-power devices, as the capacity is predominantly derived from the low-voltage region (&lt;0.2&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0008_R_d126986011e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, developing a high-capacity, superior rate-performance soft/hard composite carbon anode remains a significant challenge for advancing the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</p>
         
         <p>Currently, the sodium storage mechanism of carbonaceous material anode exhibits distinct behavior and lacks effective techniques to analyze their intrinsic causes in detail. Therefore, investigating its intrinsic mechanism is crucial for enhancing ICE and developing high-capacity carbonaceous material anodes. Most studies indicated that the sodium storage mechanism of soft carbon primarily involves adsorption dominated by intercalation through in situ XRD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0009_R_d126986011e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the currently reported testing techniques are not straightforward enough to investigate sodium storage mechanisms in structurally complex hard carbons. Initially, Dahn and Stevens proposed a model involving sodium intercalation in the slope region and microporous filling in the low-pressure region, supported through small and wide-angle X-ray scattering analysis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0010_R_d126986011e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, researchers reached a contradictory conclusion, attributing the capacity of the slope region to surface defect adsorption and identifying intercalation behavior in the low-pressure region through ex situ XRD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0011_R_d126986011e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, the adsorption-intercalation/filling model has been reported, illustrating that interlayer insertion and filling occur concurrently in the slope region through operando X-ray diffraction, ex situ Raman spectroscopy, and NMR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0012_R_d126986011e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, there is controversy among researchers regarding the observation of the characteristic peak of <sup>23</sup>Na by solid-state NMR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0013_R_d126986011e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon J. Power Sources">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, few researchers have directly observed quasi-Na metal clusters and crystal system transformation characteristic peaks by in situ XRD.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we directly observed a distinctive storage sodium mechanism of adsorption quasi-Na metallic clusters and crystal system transformation by the in situ XRD coupling with ex situ TEM. In detail, the HDCS anode uniformly adsorbs Na<sup>+</sup> in the high-voltage slope region, resulting in the formation of small particle hexagonal quasi-Na metallic clusters. Subsequently, the HDCS anode transformed the small particle hexagonal systems clusters into large particle orthorhombic systems of quasi-Na metallic clusters in the low-voltage region. Owing to such distinctive mechanisms, HDCS exhibits high specific capacity and long lifespan.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410673-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410673-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410673-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design of Highly Defective Ultrathin Carbon Nanosheets</h3>
            
            <p>The synthesis of HDCS is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). During the hydrothermal reaction, gas-phase silica reacts with zinc (Zn) ions, forming layered zinc silicate. Gluconate ions become trapped within the zinc silicate layers due to electrostatic interactions and the minimization of surface energy, resulting in the fabrication of a raw carbon layer. The zinc silicate layer and the raw carbon layer combine to form a 3D nanoflower-like structure during high-temperature carbonization. Finally, after etching in hydrofluoric acid (HF), the domain-limited HDCS was obtained. HDCS-8, HDCS-13, and HDCS-15 refer to carbonization at 800&nbsp;°C, 1300&nbsp;°C, and 1500&nbsp;°C respectively. Similarly, 3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) and gluconic acid (D-GA) were carbonized at temperatures of 800&nbsp;°C, 1300&nbsp;°C, and 1500&nbsp;°C to obtain PTCDA-8, PTCDA-13, PTCDA-15, D-GA-8, D-GA-13, and D-GA-15, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0014_R_d126986011e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1a</a>, the HDCS-8 with a ring-like structure featuring soft carbon structures with narrow layer spacing in the outer region, and a central core made up of highly defective hard carbon. HDCS-8 is surrounded by soft carbon with narrow layer spacing and fast dynamic characteristics, serving as a screening and acceleration zone for Na<sup>+</sup>. This unique structure facilitates the uniform and rapid adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> onto the hard carbon material. In detail, the HDCS-8 anode uniformly self-adsorbs Na<sup>+</sup> in the initial activation step, resulting in the formation of hexagonal quasi-Na metallic clusters. Subsequently, the HDCS-8 anode uniformly adsorbs Na<sup>+</sup> in a high-voltage slope region, resulting in the formation of many small particles of hexagonal quasi-Na metallic clusters. Finally, the HDCS-8 anode transformed the small particle hexagonal systems clusters into large particle orthorhombic systems of quasi-Na metallic clusters in the low-voltage region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/db349760-ab96-46e1-a3bc-114867c54e4f/adma202410673-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/db349760-ab96-46e1-a3bc-114867c54e4f/adma202410673-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e4b82818-a6ce-4d40-9ddd-518a3b5c9f10/adma202410673-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/db349760-ab96-46e1-a3bc-114867c54e4f/adma202410673-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis and characterization of HDCS-8. a) Schematic illustration, b) SEM, and c–e) TEM images of HDCS-8. f) Nitrogen desorption–adsorption and g) pore size distribution of nine carbon materials. h) Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) patterns of HDCS-8, PTCDA-15, and D-GA-15 counterparts.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) photographs in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1b–e</a> illustrate the morphology of HDCS-8. First, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1b,c</a> presents a 3D sheet-like nanoflower structure. As shown in the TEM images from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1d,e</a>, the surface of the soft carbon nanosheets is surrounded by hard carbon forming ring-like structures originating from fumed silica in the reaction process. Moreover, the thickness of the HDCS-8 soft carbon layered was about 3&nbsp;nm as shown in the high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), with the increase in carbonization temperature, the structure of HDCS is more like soft carbon, and the carbon layer spacing gradually decreases. Additionally, the pore structure and Brunauer-Emmett-Tellerc (BET) surface area of a series of carbon materials are characterized by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption curves. Illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1f,g</a>, the HDCS-8 boasts a substantial BET-specific surface area of up to 411 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and features an abundance of microporous structures (approximately 0.5&nbsp;nm in size).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0015_R_d126986011e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SAXS is utilized as a method to study the attributes of closed pores. The pore structure of HDCS-8 is more riches compared to that of D-GA-15 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0001">1h</a>, exhibiting a microporous structure comparable to that of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0016_R_d126986011e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summary, HDCS-8 benefits from its abundant hierarchical pore structure and large specific surface area (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>). Therefore, increasing the contact area of HDCS-8 with the electrolyte accelerates the Na<sup>+</sup> self-adsorption and transport rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0017_R_d126986011e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Microporous Mesoporous Mater. Carbon">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To further investigate the structure of the HDCS, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman tests were employed. The XRD patterns in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0002">2a,c</a> reveals characteristic peaks at approximately 23° (002) and corresponding peaks at around 43° (100) crystal faces, indicating the synthesis of amorphous carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0018_R_d126986011e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, D-GA and PTCDA carbon materials showed a significant right shift of the (002) crystal plane diffraction peak with increasing carbonization temperature. This may be attributed to the intensified graphitization and increased interplanar crystal spacing of the carbon materials. As it is well known, with increasing temperature, the characteristic peaks associated with the (002) crystal face of the carbon material shift to a higher degree, indicating a reduction in layer spacing. When sintered at 800&nbsp;°C temperature, the XRD patterns of HDCS-8 materials resemble those of hard carbon. As the temperature continuously increases, the graphitization tendency of HDCS becomes increasingly prominent, transitioning towards a structure more akin to soft carbon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0002">2e</a>). The R-value of the hard carbon decreases progressively with rising temperature in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), while the R-value of the soft carbon and HDCS increases gradually with temperature, indicating a higher degree of structural order and longer graphite bands. This trend is visually shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0002">2f</a>. The <i>R</i>-value is a parameter for evaluating the degree of disorder of carbon materials, indicating the number of carbon layers along the c-axis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0019_R_d126986011e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Carbon">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, peak fitting was conducted on the characteristic peaks around 23°, separating them into amorphous characteristic peaks at a lower degree (21.3°) and graphitized characteristic peaks at a higher degree (25.5°). This enabled the determination of the proportion corresponding to the graphitized characteristic peaks. This analysis confirms that HDCS is a carbon material possessing both soft and hard carbon structures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3e87785b-abf6-4e6a-9841-aac427648a58/adma202410673-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3e87785b-abf6-4e6a-9841-aac427648a58/adma202410673-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7cad4e0d-1b7a-4a6b-bf98-a1d69897d9a1/adma202410673-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3e87785b-abf6-4e6a-9841-aac427648a58/adma202410673-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Raman patterns, c) peak fit results of (002) crystal face diffraction peaks, and d) peak splitting fit of Raman curves a series of carbon materials. e) Results of percentage of graphitized<sub>,</sub> f) the calculation of <i>R</i>-values, and g) data curves of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> for a series of carbon materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The microstructure of the carbon material was examined using Raman spectroscopy. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0002">2b,d</a>, the Raman spectrum was analyzed for peak splitting, which revealed four peaks: T, D, D', and G. The area ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of the D to the G peak evaluates the degree of graphitization of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0011_R_d126986011e744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0002">2g</a>, similar to the XRD results, the structure of HDCS is closer to that of hard carbon material at low temperatures, while with increasing temperature, its structure shifts towards that of soft carbon.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410673-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Storage Performance of HDCS-8 Anode</h3>
            
            <p>With its substantial specific surface area and abundant hierarchical porous structure, the HDCS-8 anode is anticipated to exhibit excellent electrochemical performance. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of HDCS-8 for the first to fourth cycles at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). In the 1st scan, an irreversible peak is observed, primarily attributed to the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) resulting from electrolyte decomposition. Afterward, it is observed that the CV curves after two cycles overlap, indicating a higher reversibility for the HDCS-8 anode in SIBs. Here, a comparison is made with HDCS-8 mainly with PTCDA-15 as soft carbon and D-GA-15 as hard carbon anodes. The GCDCs were tested for the initial cycle of HDCS-8, PTCDA-15, and D-GA-15 at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0003">3a</a>). In contrast, the HDCS-8 anode exhibited a higher ICE, which was much higher than the ICE of PTCDA-15 and D-GA-15 anodes. As illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), a comparison was made between the SEI film formation states of HDCS-8 and D-GA-15 anodes upon initial discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V. It was observed that the HDCS-8 anode exhibited a more uniform thickness of the SEI film, measuring approximately 10&nbsp;nm, while the D-GA-15 anode displayed a SEI film with a uniform thickness ranging from 12 to 45&nbsp;nm.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9aba35dd-4854-40ac-a546-10b520b2478c/adma202410673-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9aba35dd-4854-40ac-a546-10b520b2478c/adma202410673-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44e8c370-8f35-4df8-973e-869775e61dc8/adma202410673-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9aba35dd-4854-40ac-a546-10b520b2478c/adma202410673-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of HDCS-8 anode in SIBs. a) Comparison of initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (GCDC) and initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), b) cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and rate performance. Long-life cycle performance at d) 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and e) 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (activation with low current before high current testing).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Additionally, as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7–S10</a> (Supporting Information), the HDCS-8 demonstrated a discernible characteristic peak indicative of quasi-Na metallic clusters after the initial resting step, whereas D-GA-15 did not exhibit a distinct sodium characteristic peak. The XPS results of the fresh electrode of HDCS-8 and the results after discharge remained the same, reaffirming the high sodium ion adsorption energy of HDCS-8 as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11–S13</a> (Supporting Information). The gradual capacity increase observed in HDCS-8 during cycling suggests a potential requirement for the progressive activation of its extensive specific surface area and abundant hierarchical open/closed pore structure.</p>
            
            <p>The GCDC was recorded for the first to three cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). When considering D-GA as a hard carbon material, the GCDC exhibits distinct high potential (&gt;0.2&nbsp;V) slope regions and low potential (&lt;0.2&nbsp;V) plateau regions. The plateau region gradually expands with increasing carbonization temperature, from 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (D-GA-8) to 283 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (D-GA-13) to 286 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (D-GA-15). In the case of PTCDA as a soft carbon material, the GCDC solely manifests a slope region, with a capacity decrease as the carbonization temperature rises, from 216 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (PTCDA-8) to 160 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (PTCDA-13) to 147 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (PTCDA-15). Notably, the GCDC behavior of HDCS anodes more closely resembles that of soft carbon, with a capacity decrease observed with increasing temperature, from 295 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (HDCS-8) to 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (HDCS-13) to 154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (HDCS-15). Consequently, the galvanostatic charge-discharge behavior of HDCS anodes resembles that of soft carbon, while its sodium storage mechanism and high-capacity performance exhibit similarities to high-defect hard carbon materials.</p>
            
            <p>The rate performance of a series of carbon materials was assessed at current densities from 0.1 to 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0003">3b</a>, it is evident that hard carbon exhibits a high capacity at low currents, but its specific capacity rapidly decreases as the current increases. This decline is attributed to the platform characteristic of hard carbon, reflecting a sluggish electrochemical reaction and subpar rate performance. Conversely, soft carbon materials demonstrate faster reaction rates due to the presence of capacity-only slope regions. While soft carbon materials boast excellent rate performance owing to rapid surface and defect adsorption, their limited capacity poses a challenge for practical applications. HDCS-8, characterized by the presence of graphitization at its edges showcases superior rate performance. As the current density increases from 0.1 to 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of HDCS-8 decreases from 296 to 177 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, showcasing a high-capacity retention of 59.8%. The GCDC corresponding to the rate test of the HDCS-8 anode is depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), illustrating the maintenance of a robust performance across the stringent current range from 0.1 to 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>The cycling stability of this series of carbon materials was investigated through cycling tests conducted at current densities of 0.1, 1, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as elucidated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0003">3c–e</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). HDCS-8 anode demonstrates a high capacity of 364 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The HDCS-8 anode reversible capacity maintained up to 277 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (capacity retention of about 100%), the HDCS-8 exhibits a high-capacity retention of 258 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (retention rate of about 94%) after 600 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. And the electrochemical performance comparison with some reported representative carbon anodes is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The gradual rise in initial cycling capacity for HDCS-8 anode may be attributed to the process of gradual activation, especially the more cycles required for activation with increasing current density in SIBs. Therefore, the 3D sheet-like nanoflower structure of HDCS-8 is designed to combine the stability characteristics of soft carbon with the high-capacity attributes of hard carbon.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the practical application of the HDCS-8 anode, the full cell with HDCS-8 as an anode and Na(Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) as a cathode (denoted as HDCS-8||NFM full cell) was also assembled, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a> (Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a> (Supporting Information) presents the GCDC of the HDCS-8 anode, NFM cathode, and full cell after the 5th cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c</a> (Supporting Information), the HDCS-8||NFM full cell retains 258.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with approximately 100% capacity retention rate. This result indicates the potential practical application of the HDCS-8 anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410673-sec-0050-title">2.3 Analysis of the Kinetic Properties of a Series of Carbon</h3>
            
            <p>To delve deeper into the excellent kinetic performance of HDCS-8, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) analyses were employed. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0004">4a,b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) illustrate the Nyquist plots and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) values for a series of carbon materials. Generally, the semicircular diameter observed in the low-frequency region represents the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, while the slope of the straight line reflects the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> within the carbon material in the high-frequency region. It is observed that the semicircle diameter and the slope of the straight line for HDCS-8 are maximized in both the low and high-frequency regions. This indicates exceptionally fast surface and internal diffusion kinetics within the HDCS-8 material. Similarly to the above results, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0004">4c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information), the GITT analysis resulted in slower ion diffusion for hard carbon and faster ion diffusion for soft carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0020_R_d126986011e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of HDCS is comparable to that of soft carbon. It is important to highlight that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate of the D-GA anode experiences a rapid decrease in the plateau region below 0.2&nbsp;V, while the ion diffusion rate of the PTCDA and HDCS anode does not decrease rapidly as they operate within the slope region. The exceptional rate performance of HDCS-8 anode may be attributed to the fact that hard carbon mainly performs hole filling, but soft carbon usually entails Na<sup>+</sup> intercalated in the low-voltage region. As a result, the kinetics of soft carbon, associated with the intercalated type of sodium storage, are superior to the adsorption-filled type of hard carbon. Therefore, the soft carbon kinetics of the intercalated type of sodium storage is better than the adsorption-filled type of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0021_R_d126986011e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process, CV curves of HDCS-8 at 0.1–2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0004">4d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0022_R_d126986011e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater. Small">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pseudo-capacitance contribution enhances from 81.1% to 94% 0.1–2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), thus suggesting that surface adsorption dominates for sodium storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6abb2f07-b9c4-474e-80bb-0a041626ba6e/adma202410673-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6abb2f07-b9c4-474e-80bb-0a041626ba6e/adma202410673-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0e6ff8ce-a4aa-4094-8f2b-1ca32e4ba4e7/adma202410673-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6abb2f07-b9c4-474e-80bb-0a041626ba6e/adma202410673-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic analysis performance of a series of carbon materials. a) Electrochemical impedance spectra, b) <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, and c) calculated values of ionic diffusion by GITT of a series of carbon materials. d) CV curves of HDCS-8 with different scan rates. e) The ratio of capacitance contribution at different scan rates of HDCS-8. f) The Na nucleation overpotentials of a series of carbon materials at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To achieve an in-depth understanding of the Na storage mechanism, it is crucial to investigate the galvanostatic discharge curves up to the point where the characteristic “V” shape is reached (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0004">4f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). This “V” shape signifies the Na nucleation barrier, suggesting the discharge state where a series of carbon materials achieve the maximum reversible capacity (over discharge 0 &lt;V was applied to Na half cells).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0003_R_d126986011e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202410673-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0021_R_d126986011e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results show that the nucleation barrier of the HDCS-8 anode (-2.4&nbsp;mV) is comparable to the soft carbon PTCDA-15 (-2.6&nbsp;mV) and lower than that of D-GA hard carbon anodes. The Na nucleation barrier of the HDCS anodes remains relatively consistent across different carbonization temperatures. Conversely, the D-GA and PTCDA anodes exhibit a reduction in the Na nucleation barrier with increasing temperature. Furthermore, consistent with the GITT and EIS calculations, HDCS-8 demonstrates similarity to soft carbon and lower values than hard carbon in terms of the Na nucleation barrier. As a result, it is further demonstrated that HDCS-8 has a kinetic performance comparable to that of soft carbon, thus presenting excellent rate performance, high capacity, and long-cycle performance.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the cause of excellent electrochemical performance for the HDCS-8 anode. DFT calculations were employed to compare the DOS distribution, adsorption energy, and diffusion energy barriers of PTCDA-15 soft carbon, HDCS-8 (soft/hard carbon), and D-GA-15 hard carbon anodes. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0005">5a–c</a>, it is noted that soft carbon, similar to graphene, possesses low defects and is electrically conductive. However, the height of the electronic states near the Fermi energy level is relatively lower, which limits its electronic conductivity. In contrast, hard carbon, rich in defects, exhibits an altered electronic structure with increased electronic states near the Fermi energy level, leading to improved electrical conductivity. However, the abundance of defects in hard carbon may compromise its structural stability. Finally, for the soft/hard carbon, the increased electronic states near the Fermi energy level offer enhanced conductivity compared to soft carbon with low defects. Additionally, the coating of soft carbon enhances the structural stability of highly defective hard carbon.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6214f4e6-97d0-4091-aa2c-48ef0a9d3ddd/adma202410673-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6214f4e6-97d0-4091-aa2c-48ef0a9d3ddd/adma202410673-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44c6a10b-8a0c-4c39-af9a-c95c6c6fd789/adma202410673-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6214f4e6-97d0-4091-aa2c-48ef0a9d3ddd/adma202410673-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculation of PTCDA-15 Soft carbon, D-GA-15 Hard carbon, and HDCS-8 soft/hard carbon anodes in SIBs. a–c) Comparison of density of states (DOS). d) Comparative surface layer adsorption energy. Comparison e,g) surface and f,h) internal diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The specific surface-active sites of HDCS-8 play a crucial role in influencing its capacity performance. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a–c</a> (Supporting Information), DFT calculations were utilized to determine the surface adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup>. The Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy of hard carbon (-2.0316&nbsp;eV) exhibited a higher adsorption energy compared to soft carbon (-1.2798&nbsp;eV), with soft/hard carbon (-1.5790&nbsp;eV) displaying an intermediate adsorption energy state. Consequently, HDCS-8, as a soft/hard composite carbon, demonstrates a higher capacity than soft carbon and better rate performance than hard carbon. The diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> was investigated to understand its impact on rate performance by examining the diffusion energy barriers. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0005">5e,g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22d–f</a> (Supporting Information), the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion occurred more rapidly on the hard carbon surface (0.2998&nbsp;eV) than on the soft carbon surface (0.4136&nbsp;eV), while for the soft/hard carbon (0.3285&nbsp;eV), an intermediate barrier was observed. The inner atomic diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0005">5f,h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22g–i</a> (Supporting Information), and the overall results are consistent with the surface diffusion trend. In conclusion, when considering the sodium storage mechanisms, hard carbon exhibits higher adsorption energy and lower diffusion barriers compared to soft carbon. Soft/hard carbons fall in between, exhibiting high-rate performance and high-capacity cycling stability. However, it is worth noting that the DFT calculations show that hard carbon is better than soft carbon, while the kinetic experiments show that soft carbon is superior to hard carbon, which is mainly because the soft carbon belongs to the adsorption insertion, while the hard carbon belongs to the adsorption-filled storage of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0021_R_d126986011e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The kinetics of sodium adsorption storage is superior to that of sodium adsorption-filled storage.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410673-sec-0060-title">2.4 Sodium Storage Mechanism of Adsorption Quasi-Na Metallic Clusters and Crystal System Transformation</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium storage mechanisms of carbon materials, particularly hard carbon and soft/hard composite carbon, have been subject to various interpretations. The sodium storage behaviors of HDCS-8, PTCDA-15, and D-GA-15 anodes were compared using in situ XRD. All the in situ XRD data were collected at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Initially, the results of the in situ XRD test for the HDCS-8 anode and the corresponding GCDC are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0006">6a</a>. During the 2nd discharging process, the observed characteristic peaks corresponded to the (100) and (101) crystal faces of hexagonal system (HS) quasi-Na metal clusters (PDF#97-004-4758) within the discharge range of 0.7–3.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0023_R_d126986011e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Acta Crystallogr.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strong characteristic peak of quasi-Na metal is notable here, attributed to the high Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy of HDCS-8, leading to the formation of clusters of quasi-Na metal clusters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0013_R_d126986011e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon J. Power Sources">13</a>, <a href="#adma202410673-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0024_R_d126986011e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the discharge progressed into the range of 0.3–0.7&nbsp;V, the characteristic peaks associated with the (101) crystal face disappeared, while the characteristic peaks of the (002) crystal face became evident. With discharge to the range of 0.3–0.7&nbsp;V, the characteristic peaks of the (101) crystal face disappeared, and the characteristic peaks of the (002) crystal face were observed. As the discharge proceeded into the low-voltage range (0.01–0.3&nbsp;V), the quasi-Na metallic clusters transformed HS into the orthorhombic system (OS) (PDF#97-018-9460), with the detection of (002) and (011) crystal faces. During the 2nd charging process, characteristic peaks of the quasi-Na metal clusters in the OS (002) and (011) crystal faces were still evident within the voltage range of 0.01–1.75&nbsp;V. Upon charging to the range of 1.75–3.0&nbsp;V, the quasi-Na metal clusters transitioned from OS to HS, with only (100) and (002) facets being observed. The in situ XRD results of the initial cycle of HDCS-8, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), remain consistent with the second cycle. In summary, HDCS-8 demonstrates an adsorption-crystal system transformation sodium storage mechanism, wherein the quasi-Na metal clusters exhibit a hexagonal system (HS) in the high-voltage region that transitions to an orthorhombic system (OS) in the low-voltage region. Furthermore, the in situ XRD data from the two cycles of HDCS-8 indicate that the entire process of sodium adsorption and crystalline transformation is reversible. This highlights that the high capacity of HDCS-8 is attributed to the crystal system transformation at low voltage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/208ccf08-e04a-4847-b15b-ec55b6b23c51/adma202410673-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/208ccf08-e04a-4847-b15b-ec55b6b23c51/adma202410673-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/78e622ef-50e3-4890-8ae5-f366b048e6a5/adma202410673-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/208ccf08-e04a-4847-b15b-ec55b6b23c51/adma202410673-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The sodium storage mechanism of HDCS-8 anode. a) In situ XRD results and corresponding GCDC of HDCS-8 anode at the second cycles. b) The optical photograph of the HDCS-8 electrode discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V soaked in the ethanol solution containing 1% phenolphthalein. c) Differential Scanning Calorimetry (DSC) profiles of HDCS-8 anode initial electrode and discharge to 1.5, 0.5, and 0.01&nbsp;V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on the adsorption-crystalline system transformation mechanism, the composition of quasi-Na metal clusters in HDCS-8 electrode materials at different discharge voltages was analyzed. The optical photograph of HDCS-8 electrodes discharged to 1.5, 0.5, and 0.01&nbsp;V, and subsequently soaked in an ethanol solution containing 1% phenolphthalein (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0006">6b</a>). It is noteworthy that the solution exhibits a significantly darker color when discharged to 0.5&nbsp;V compared to 1.5&nbsp;V, attributed to the presence of adsorbed quasi-Na metal clusters (HS). However, when discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, there is no significant color change observed compared to 0.5&nbsp;V. At low voltages, quasi-Na metal clusters (HS) cease to be adsorbed, instead undergoing transformation into quasi-Na metal clusters (OS). Likewise, we compared the color after changes of PTCDA-15 and D-GA-15 electrodes discharged to the same voltage, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). With regard to the PTCDA-15 soft carbon electrodes, the overall color change is not readily discernible. This observation is attributed to the fact that the primary Na<sup>+</sup> enters the interlayer spacing of the carbon material rather than forming quasi-Na metal clusters. For D-GA-15 hard carbon electrodes, the color change in the solution is not pronounced when discharged to 1.5 and 0.5&nbsp;V. However, a clear red color is observed when discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, mainly because the capacity contribution of hard carbon is significant at low voltages. The presence of crystal system transformation in the quasi-Na metal clusters was further confirmed by DSC analysis. An appreciable alteration in the heat absorption peak is observed between 70 and 100&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0006">6c</a>). When discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V, the heat absorption peak reaches its maximum, decreasing steadily with continued discharge to 0.5&nbsp;V. Upon full discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the heat absorption peak closely aligns with that of the initial electrode. This shift in the heat absorption peak indicated the heat absorption characteristics of quasi-Na metal clusters (HS), which nearly vanish upon discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V as the quasi-Na metal clusters are fully transformed into OS. In addition, the solid-state NMR spectra of the fresh electrodes, electrodes contacting with the electrolyte at the initial state, and discharged to 1.5, 0.5, and 0.01&nbsp;V states were shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). The characteristic peaks of sodium quasi-Na metallic clusters were observed at 680, 1110, and 1140&nbsp;ppm when the HDCS-8 anode discharged to 1.5, 0.5, and 0.01&nbsp;V, respectively. The characteristic peaks of sodium quasi-Na metallic clusters gradually positively shifted with the discharging, which confirms more formation of quasi-Na metallic clusters. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> characteristic peaks were observed at 5 and 80&nbsp;ppm when discharged to 1.5, 0.5, and 0.01&nbsp;V, and the peak intensities were gradually strengthened, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation or adsorption may accompany the discharge process. Therefore, the distinctive adsorption-crystalline system transformation mechanism of HDCS-8 is further substantiated through color contrast experiments, solid-state NMR, and DSC tests.</p>
            
            <p>To further investigate the sodium storage mechanism of the HDCS-8 anode, we conducted an ex situ TEM analysis to confirm the adsorption-crystal system transformation sodium storage mechanism. Upon discharging to 0.5&nbsp;V, a significant presence of small quasi-Na metal nanoparticles adsorbed on the HDCS-8 was observed, as depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7a</a>. Upon further magnification, TEM images revealed that numerous small quasi-Na metal nanoparticles were distributed in a ring-like structure. In the ring-like structure, the outer layer composed of soft carbon can act as a screening size and acceleration zone for Na<sup>+</sup>, due to its narrow layer spacing and fast dynamic properties. This characteristic helps to rapid and uniform adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> within the central hard carbon. As elucidated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7b</a>, many quasi-Na metal particles (diameter about 2–6&nbsp;nm) are uniformly distributed on the surface of HDCS-8. Further examination of the HR-TEM image reveals that it corresponds to the (101) crystal surface of the HS quasi-Na metal clusters. When discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, larger and more aggregated quasi-Na metal clusters are observed as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7c</a>. The HR-TEM image reveals that the quasi-Na metal clusters are significantly larger and exhibit the (011) crystal surface of the OS quasi-Na metal clusters, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7d</a>. It is noteworthy that distinct alignments of the same crystal face within the same region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7d1,d2</a>), attributed to the transformation of multiple small HS quasi-Na metal cluster particles into OS quasi-Na metal clusters.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410673-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1728245-d546-4613-ae58-56a90541c56e/adma202410673-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1728245-d546-4613-ae58-56a90541c56e/adma202410673-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/876a0e9e-9b5a-4d1b-a3de-7bf3cae3fd8f/adma202410673-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1728245-d546-4613-ae58-56a90541c56e/adma202410673-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410673-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410673" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The sodium storage mechanism of HDCS-8 anode. TEM and corresponding elemental mapping images of HDCS-8 anode discharged to a) 0.5&nbsp;V and c) 0.01&nbsp;V. TEM and corresponding HR-TEM images of HDCS-8 anode discharged to b) 0.01&nbsp;V and d) 0.5&nbsp;V. Schematic illustration of the sodium storage mechanism of HDCS-8 anode. e) Schematic diagram of sodium storage mechanism of HDCS-8 anode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, as illustrated in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), it was preliminarily verified that there were no obvious changes in the characteristic peaks of O 1s, Na 1s, F 1s, and C 1s when discharging to 0.01 and 0.5&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410673-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410673-bib-0025_R_d126986011e1230" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation suggests that there is no alteration in the valence state of Na at low voltages, instead of Na crystal system transformation likely occurs. The HDCS-8 anode was tested to characterize the morphology of the many cycles of discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V. The structure of the nanosheet layer of HDCS-8 remains intact, with the elemental distributions showing a uniform distribution of C and Na as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). This further validates the structural stability of HDCS-8. Moreover, electrodes of HDCS-8 and D-GA-15 were compared after 500 cycles of discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information)reveals that the surface of D-GA-15 exhibits a distinct white region indicative of Na<sup>+</sup> clusters, whereas HDCS-8 remains flat and smooth. This difference may be attributed to the lack of a rich porous structure and larger S<sub>BET</sub> on the surface of the D-GA-15 anode, leading to sodium deposition. In contrast, the HDCS-8 anode features a hierarchical porous structure, 2D sheet structure, and a unique ring-like configuration that helps to uniform for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, thereby preserving the electrode structure and resulting in a flat and smooth surface without prominent quasi-Na metal clusters regions.</p>
            
            <p>The sodium storage mechanism of the HDCS-8 anode has been demonstrated through a combined analysis. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410673-fig-0007">7e</a>, the 3D nano-flower structure of HDCS-8 offers a large specific surface area, providing numerous reaction sites. The rich hierarchical porous structure, encompassing both open and closed pores, enhances Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capacity. The distinctive adsorption-crystal system transformation mechanism of sodium storage in HDCS-8 contributes to improved capacity performance. Additionally, the soft/hard carbon ring-like distribution structure of HDCS-8 enables comparable kinetic performance to soft carbon, excellent structural stability during charge and discharge cycles, and enhances the rate and long-term stability performance of sodium storage.</p>
            
            <p>The HDCS-8 is a carbon material that encompasses both soft and hard carbon components, showcasing a distinctive adsorption-crystalline system transformation mechanism for sodium storage. To differentiate between the traditional hard and soft carbon sodium storage mechanisms, a comparison was made between the sodium storage mechanisms of D-GA-15 and PTCDA-15 anodes using in situ XRD. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), which displays the in situ XRD and corresponding GCDC of PTCDA-15 soft carbon, it was observed that the (002) crystal surface gradually shifted towards a lower degree direction during the discharge process, indicating Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the carbon structure. In the case of PTCDA-15 soft carbon, the gradual shift of the (002) crystal surface toward a lower degree during charging signifies the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the carbon layer structure. Conversely, during charging, the gradual shift of the (002) crystal surface toward a higher degree direction indicates the detachment of Na<sup>+</sup> from the carbon layer. Here, for PTCDA-15 soft carbon the characteristic peak of (002) exhibits a left shift and lasts longer during the second cycling process. This may be because the carbon layer spacing was enlarged during the initial sodium embedding process. Thus, PTCDA-15 soft carbon primarily undergoes a Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process for sodium storage. On the other hand, as observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the (002) crystal surface of the D-GA-15 hard carbon anode does not exhibit significant shifts during charging and discharging, and no other distinct characteristic peaks are evident. This suggests that D-GA-15 hard carbon mainly undergoes an adsorption-filling process for sodium storage. Therefore, the distinctive adsorption-crystal system transformation sodium storage mechanism of the HDCS-8 anode electrode was effectively demonstrated through color comparison, DSC, ex situ TEM, and in situ XRD tests.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410673-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410673-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we directly observed a distinctive sodium storage mechanism of adsorbed quasi-Na metallic clusters and crystal system transformations in the HDCS-8 anode in situ XRD coupling with ex situ TEM. In detail, during the discharge process, small hexagonal system quasi-Na metallic clusters are initially formed at higher voltages, followed by their transformation into larger orthorhombic system quasi-Na metallic clusters at lower voltages. Furthermore, we have elucidated the sodium storage mechanisms of soft carbon and hard carbon anodes. Soft carbon and hard carbon operate through adsorption-insertion and adsorption-filling sodium storage mechanisms, respectively. This distinctive sodium storage mechanism exhibited by HDCS-8 enables exceptional performance. The HDCS-8 anode demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 364 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 100% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Therefore, such distinctive adsorption of quasi-Na metallic clusters and crystalline system transformation mechanism for sodium storage presents a promising avenue for the future development of carbonaceous anode materials with extended lifespans and high capacity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410673-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410673-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410673-sec-0110-title"> The Synthesis of HDCS-8, HDCS-13, and HDCS-15</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, 0.2&nbsp;g of fumed silica and 150&nbsp;mL of deionized water were homogeneously dispersed by sonication and magnetic stirring. Then, 1.0&nbsp;g of zinc gluconate (ZG), was added to the above solution. After complete dissolution, transfer it to a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave at 200&nbsp;°C for 12 h. The reacted solution was washed and dried to obtain a yellow powder, carbonized at 800&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> to obtain Zn<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>/C composite materials. Zn<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>/C composite materials were stirred with hydrofluoric acid for 4 h at room temperature to remove the Zn<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>/C phase to obtain domain-limited highly defective ultrathin carbon nanosheets (HDCS-8). Finally, to further investigate the structural changes of carbon materials at different carbonization temperatures, HDCS was further carbonized at 1300&nbsp;°C and 1500&nbsp;°C, named HDCS-13 and HDCS-15, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410673-sec-0120-title"> The Synthesis of D-GA-8, D-GA-13, D-GA-15</h4>
                  
                  <p>10&nbsp;mL of D-gluconic acid solution was homogeneously dispersed into 75&nbsp;mL of deionized water and then reacted in a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave at 200&nbsp;°C keep 12 h. The hydrothermal products were further carbonized at 800&nbsp;°C, 1300&nbsp;°C, and 1500&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h to obtain typical hard carbon materials named D-GA-8, D-GA-13, and D-GA-15.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410673-sec-0130-title"> The Synthesis of PTCDA-8, PTCDA-13, and PTCDA-15</h4>
                  
                  <p>3,4,9,10-Perylenetetracarboxylic dianhydride was carbonized at 800&nbsp;°C, 1300&nbsp;°C and 1500&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h to obtain typical soft carbon materials named PTCDA-8, PTCDA-13, and PTCDA-15.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410673-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410673-sec-0140-title"> Statistical Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The GITT and charge–discharge performance measurements of the batteries (assembled into 2032 coin-type cells, purchased from Canrd) were tested on a NEWARE Battery Test System (Shenzhen China) and LAND CT2001A battery tester (Wuhan, China). EIS and CV measurements (amplitude: 5&nbsp;mV; Frequency: 0.01&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz) were conducted on electrochemical workstation (DongHua Analytical DH7006B and Eco Chemie Autolab B.V. (Metrohm, Switzerland)). The above data were obtained by direct testing. The calculation methods of the obtained data were described in Supporting Information in detail, and the processing softwares were used by the commercial PowerPoint and Origin.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
